[b][color=red]Claim:[\/color][\/b]  	We have $f(x^2) = f(x)^2$ for all $x \in {\mathbb Q}_{>0}$.

[i]Proof.[\/i]  	Letting $P(x,y)$ denote the given statement, 	plug in $P(f(1)^{-1}, 1)$ to deduce $f(f(1)^{-1}) = 1$. 	Then $P(x, f(1)^{-1})$ finishes. $\blacksquare$

Now, write the given as \[ f(y) = \left[ \frac{f(xf(y))}{f(x)} \right]^2. \] We prove by induction on $n \ge 0$ than all elements of $\operatorname{Img} f$ are $2^n$'th powers for each $n$. The base case $n = 0$ is vacuous, and the inductive step follows by above identity. It follows we must have $f$ equal to $1$ always. Okay, the end.

Determine all functions $f:\mathbb{Q}_{>0}\to \mathbb{Q}_{>0}$ satisfying $$f(x^2f(y)^2)=f(x)^2f(y)$$ for all $x,y\in\mathbb{Q}_{>0}$[\/quote]

[b]Solution[\/b] Plugging $x = \frac{1}{f(1)}, y = 1$ gives us $f\left(\frac{1}{f(1)}\right) = 1$. Plugging $x, \frac{1}{f(1)}$ gives us $f(x^2) = f(x)^2$. This gives us $f(xf(y))^2 = f(x)^2f(y)$ or $f(y)$ is a square of a rational for all positive rationals $q$. Now plug $f_1(x) = \sqrt{f(x)}$ to get $f_1(xf_1(y)^2)^4 = f_1(x)^4f_1(y)^2$ which again gives $f_1(y)$ is a square of a rational for all positive rationals $q$. <span style="white-space:pre;"><img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/7\/6\/c\/76c6026d0088fdba93b24f90dc82675a3151c397.png" class="latex" alt="$f\equiv 1$" style="vertical-align: -3px" width="43" height="16" >,<\/span> as desired.","post_canonical":"As said above, the only answer is $f\equiv 1$.

By the original equality, for all $x, y\in \mathbb{Q}_{> 0}$,

$$f(f(x))^2 f(y) = f(f(x)^2 f(y)^2) = f(f(y))^2 f(x)$$

i.e. $\frac{f(x)}{f(y)} = \left(\frac{f(f(x))}{f(f(y))}\right)^2$ for all positive rational numbers $x$ and $y$. Continuing by induction,

$$ \frac{f(x)}{f(y)} = \left(\frac{f^k(x)}{f^k(y)}\right)^{2^{k-1}} $$

for all $k\geq 1$, where $f^k$ here means $k$-fold application of $f$. This implies that $2^{k-1}|v_p\left(\frac{f(x)}{f(y)}\right)$ for every positive integer $k$ and prime numbers $p$, so $f$ must be constant, and thus $f\equiv 1$, as desired.","username":"BlazingMuddy","reported":false,"is_thanked":false,"is_nothanked":false,"attachment":false,"thanks_received":12,"nothanks_received":0,"thankers":"Dividend, pavel kozlov, Nathanisme, Pluto04, Siddharth03, centslordm, jhu08, megarnie, HoRI_DA_GRe8, ImSh95, chystudent1-_-, Adventure10","deleted":false,"post_number":5,"post_time":1563368714,"num_edits":2,"post_format":"bbcode","last_edit_time":1563368884,"last_editor_username":"BlazingMuddy","last_edit_reason":"more edits :(","admin":false,"avatar":"\/\/avatar.artofproblemsolving.com\/avatar_417515.jpg?t=1532449158","num_posts":277,"editable":false,"deletable":false,"show_from_start":true,"show_from_end":false},{"post_id":12753162,"topic_id":1876757,"poster_id":294303,"post_rendered":"<img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/8\/4\/a\/84a29dd5972b2953b705ca7875c601ad1173216b.png" class="latex" alt="$f(x)=1,  \forall x\in \mathbb{Q^+}$" style="vertical-align: -4px" width="143" height="19" ><br>

\\ $P(x,y) \rightarrow f(x^{2}f(y)^2)=f(x)^{2}f(y) $
\\ $P(1,x) \rightarrow f(f(x)^2)=f(1)^{2}f(x)...(A)$
\\ $P(\displaystyle \frac{x}{f(x^2)},x^2) \rightarrow f(x^2)=f(\frac{x}{f(x^2)})^{2}f(x^2)$ $\Rightarrow  f(\displaystyle \frac{x}{f(x^2)})=1$
\\ $\exists c\in \mathbb{Q^+}$ such that $f(c)=1$
\\ $P(x,c) \rightarrow f(x^2)=f(x)^2$
\\ $P(x,y^2) \rightarrow f(xf(y)^2)^2=f(x)^{2}f(y)^{2}$ $\Rightarrow f(xf(y)^2)=f(x)f(y) ... (1)$
\\ In (1) let $x=1$ and we obtain $f(f(y)^2)=f(1)f(y)...(B)$ 
\\ Combining (A) and (B) yields $f(1)=1$
\\ So $f(f(x)^2)= f(f(x))^2= f(x)$ $\Rightarrow f^k(x)=\sqrt[k]{f(x)}$ where $k$ is a power of $2$.
\\ Suppose that for some $a\in \mathbb{Q^+}$ such that $f(a)\neq 1$ let $f(a)=\displaystyle \frac{m}{n}$ such that $\gcd(m,n)=1$
\\ Let $k$ be a power of $2$ with $V_{p}(m)<k \wedge V_{p}(n)<k$ for all primes $p$.
\\ $f^k(a)=\sqrt[k]{f(a)}$ so $\sqrt[k]{f(a)} \in \mathbb{Q^+}$
\\ Let $\sqrt[k]{\displaystyle\frac{m}{n}}=\displaystyle\frac{x}{y}$ for some $x,y \in \mathbb{N}$ with $\gcd(x,y)=1$
\\ $my^k=nx^k$ if $p \mid m \Rightarrow p \mid x$
\\ $V_{p}(my^k)=V_{p}(nx^k)$ Since $\gcd(x,y)=1$ $V_{p}(y)=0$
\\ $V_{p}(my^k)=V_{p}(m)=V_{p}(x^k)=k \cdot V_{p}(x) \geq k$ this is clearly a contradiction and thus there doesn't exist any positive rational number $a$ with $f(a)\neq 1$.

It clearly does. As usual, let $P(x,y)$ denote the assertion $f(x^2f(y)^2) = f(x)^2f(y)$.
$$P\left(\frac{1}{f(1)},1\right)\implies f(\text{something})=1.$$
If $f(a)=1$, then $P(x,a)\implies f(x^2)=f(x)^2$. Thus
$$P(1,x)\implies f(x) = \left(\frac{f(f(x))}{f(1)}\right)^2\qquad (*).$$
If we denote $\mathbb{Q}^n$ by $\{q^n : q\in\mathbb{Q}\}$ then (*) implies $\mathrm{Im}(f) \subseteq \mathbb{Q}^2$. Using (*) repeatedly gives $\mathrm{Im}(f) \subseteq \mathbb{Q}^4$, $\mathrm{Im}(f) \subseteq \mathbb{Q}^8$, $\mathrm{Im}(f) \subseteq \mathbb{Q}^{16}$,...

<img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/a\/5\/7\/a57b068a77fbf18e2043bcdf208e91f20e6ec1b7.png" class="latexcenter" alt="$$2^{n-1} \vert v_p \left(\frac{f(x)}{c} \right) \Rightarrow v_p \left(\frac{f(x)}{c} \right)=0 \Rightarrow \frac{f(x)}{c}=1$$"  width="371" height="43" >Hence <img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/b\/b\/2\/bb2c93730dbb48558bb3c4738c956c4e8f816437.png" class="latex" alt="$f$" style="vertical-align: -3px" width="10" height="16" > is constant and <img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/3\/3\/7\/3372c1cb6d68cf97c2d231acc0b47b95a9ed04cc.png" class="latex" alt="$c$"  width="8" height="8" > satisfies <img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/9\/8\/b\/98b1291486220156b70937943d279d9296b84f59.png" class="latex" alt="$c=c^3$"  width="46" height="15" > so we must have <img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/0\/e\/4\/0e4cc644ec8a1c3623c13ed3d14904096a0d87b9.png" class="latex" alt="$c=1$" style="vertical-align: 0px" width="40" height="12" > hence:<br>
<img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/6\/f\/a\/6fa206b961ed62fc58fa855b1286c18ad6536218.png" class="latexcenter" alt="$$\boxed{f(x) \equiv 1}$$"  width="80" height="30" >which indeed works","post_canonical":"$$P \left(f(x),y \right) \Rightarrow f \left(f(x)^2 f(y)^2 \right)=f(f(x))^2 f(y)$$
Swapping $x,y$ and equating gives:
$$f(f(x))^2 f(y)=f(f(y))^2 f(x) \Rightarrow \frac{f(f(x))^2}{f(x)}=\frac{f(f(y))^2}{f(y)}=c$$
for some constant $c \in \mathbb{Q}_{>0}$ (as this holds $\forall x,y$). We now claim:
$$\underbrace{f \circ \cdots \circ f}_{\text{n times}}(x)=f^n(x)=c \cdot \left(\frac{f(x)}{c} \right)^{1\/2^{n-1}}$$
for all $n \geq 1$. This clearly holds for $n=1$. We go by induction. For $n \geq 2$:
$$f^{n}(x)=f(f(f^{n-2}(x)))=\left(c f(f^{n-2}(x)) \right)^{1\/2}=\left(c f^{n-1}(x) \right)^{1\/2}=\left(c^2 \left(\frac{f(x)}{c} \right)^{1\/2^{n-2}} \right)^{1\/2}$$
and expanding out shows the result is true by induction.

But then we have:
$$\left(\frac{f(x)}{c} \right)^{1\/2^{n-1}}=\frac{f^{n}(x)}{c} \in \mathbb{Q}_{>0}$$
for all $n$. But if $p$ is prime this means for all $n$:
$$2^{n-1} \vert v_p \left(\frac{f(x)}{c} \right) \Rightarrow v_p \left(\frac{f(x)}{c} \right)=0 \Rightarrow \frac{f(x)}{c}=1$$
Hence $f$ is constant and $c$ satisfies $c=c^3$ so we must have $c=1$ hence:
$$\boxed{f(x) \equiv 1}$$
which indeed works","username":"sbealing","reported":false,"is_thanked":false,"is_nothanked":false,"attachment":false,"thanks_received":4,"nothanks_received":0,"thankers":"centslordm, ImSh95, Adventure10, Mango247","deleted":false,"post_number":8,"post_time":1563386161,"num_edits":0,"post_format":"bbcode","last_edit_time":0,"last_editor_username":"","last_edit_reason":"","admin":false,"avatar":"\/\/avatar.artofproblemsolving.com\/avatar_211552.png","num_posts":305,"editable":false,"deletable":false,"show_from_start":true,"show_from_end":false},{"post_id":12760621,"topic_id":1876757,"poster_id":177841,"post_rendered":"

Let $\mathbb{Q}_{>0}$ denote the set of all positive rational numbers. Determine all functions $f:\mathbb{Q}_{>0}\to \mathbb{Q}_{>0}$ satisfying $$f(x^2f(y)^2)=f(x)^2f(y)$$for all $x,y\in\mathbb{Q}_{>0}$
[\/quote]

Let $P(x,y)$ denote the given FE. Let $k=f(1)\in\mathbb{Q}_{>0}$. Now,
\[P(x,1)\implies f((xk)^2)=f(x)^2k.\]
Plugging in $x=1\/k$ into this, we get that $k=f(1\/k)^2k$, so $f(1\/k)=1$. Now,
\[P(x,1\/k)\implies f(x^2)=f(x)^2.\]
Thus, the FE implies
\[f(y)=\left(\frac{f(xf(y))}{f(x)}\right)^2.\]

[u]Claim:[\/u] $f(y)$ is always a perfect $2^k$th power.

[u]Proof of Claim:[\/u] We proceed by induction on $k$. The case $k=0$ is trivial. Now, the equation we derived above shows that
\[f(y)=a^{2^{k+1}}\]
for some rational $a$, which completes the induction. $\blacksquare$

>.<\/span>","post_canonical":"Note that the only element $q\in \mathbb{Q}_{>0}$ which is a rational number when taken $\frac{1}{2^k}$-th power is $1$. 
Let $x=f(z)$ at the given assertion, then $f(f(z)^2f(y)^2)=f(f(z))^2f(y)=f(f(y)^2)f(z)$. Hence there exists some $c \in \mathbb{Q}_{>0}$ such that $\frac{f(f(x))^2}{f(x)}=c$ for any $x$. Now, $P(\frac{1}{f(y)},f(y)): f(\frac{1}{f(y)^2} \cdot f(y))=f(\frac{1}{f(y)})^2 \cdot \sqrt{f(y)} => f(\frac{1}{f(y)}) \cdot \sqrt{f(y)} =1=>$$f(\frac{1}{f(y)})=\frac{1}{\sqrt{f(y)})}$.

Let $c = f\left(1\right)$, and let $P\left(x, y\right)$ denote the initial assertion. From $P\left(1, 1\right)$, we have
	\begin{align*}
		f\left(c^2\right) &= c^3.
	\end{align*}
Now, from $P\left(c^2, 1\right)$, we have
	\begin{align*}
		f\left(c^6\right) &= c^7,
	\end{align*}
while from $P\left(1, c^2\right)$, we have
	\begin{align*}
		f\left(c^6\right) &= c^5.
	\end{align*}
Hence, $c^5 = c^7$, and since $c \in \mathbb{Q}_{> 0}$, $c = 1$. Therefore, $f\left(1\right) = 1$. Now, $P\left(x, 1\right)$ implies
	\begin{align*}
		f\left(x^2\right) &= f\left(x\right)^2.
	\end{align*}
From $P\left(1, x\right)$, we have
	\begin{align*}
		f\left(f\left(x\right)^2\right) &= f\left(x\right) \\
		f\left(f\left(x\right)\right)^2 &= f\left(x\right).
	\end{align*}
Therefore, if $k$ is in the image of $f$, so is $k^{\frac{1}{2}}$. Iterating, we find that in fact $k^{\frac{1}{2^\ell}}$ is in the image of $f$ for all positive integers $\ell$. Now, suppose $k \neq 1$. Then, for $\ell$ large enough, $k^{\frac{1}{2^\ell}}$ will not be rational, contradiction. Hence, we must have $k = 1$. This immediately implies that $f \equiv 1$ identically, concluding the proof. $\Box$","username":"niyu","reported":false,"is_thanked":false,"is_nothanked":false,"attachment":false,"thanks_received":3,"nothanks_received":0,"thankers":"centslordm, ImSh95, Adventure10","deleted":false,"post_number":12,"post_time":1563505215,"num_edits":0,"post_format":"bbcode","last_edit_time":0,"last_editor_username":"","last_edit_reason":"","admin":false,"avatar":"\/\/avatar.artofproblemsolving.com\/avatar_274319.png","num_posts":830,"editable":false,"deletable":false,"show_from_start":true,"show_from_end":false},{"post_id":12951098,"topic_id":1876757,"poster_id":354898,"post_rendered":"The answer is <span style="white-space:pre;"><img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/9\/7\/0\/970c2f2b191736b9dca01666288a6afed169484a.png" class="latex" alt="$f(x) =1 \ \ \forall x \in \mathbb{Q^+}$" style="vertical-align: -4px" width="146" height="19" >.<\/span><br>

Contradiction. So we are done.","post_canonical":"The answer is $f(x) =1 \ \ \forall x \in \mathbb{Q^+}$.
Plug $x=\frac{1}{f(1)^2}\overset{def}{=} t, y=1 \longrightarrow f(1)=f(t)^2 f(1) \longrightarrow f(t)=1$.
Then $y=t \longrightarrow f(x^2) = f^2(x)  \ \ \forall x$. From here, $f(1)= f^2(1) \longrightarrow f(1)=1$.
Plug $x=1 \longrightarrow f^2(f(y))=f(f^2(y))=f(y) \longrightarrow f(f(y)) = \sqrt{f(y)} \dots (1)$.  Fix a rational number $l$.

Claim: $f(l)$ is a $2^{n}$ power of a rational for all $n \in \mathbb{N}$.
Let $f^{[n]}(x) = \underbrace{f(f(\dots f(x))\dots )}_{n \ times}$
Then iterating $(1)$ we see that $$f^{[n+1]}(l) = f^{[n]}(f(l))= (f(f(l))^{\frac{1}{2^{n-1}}}=(f(l))^{\frac{1}{2^n}}$$
And so our claim is proved.

Now, suppose $f(l) \neq 1$. Then by our claim, at some point $f(l)$ is irrational. Contradiction. I claim that $f(x)=1$ is the only solution, which is easily verifiable, and I will do this by showing that $f(x)$ is a perfect $2^n$th power of a rational for all $x\in \mathbb{Q}_{>0}$. We proceed with induction on $n$. The base case is easy: taking $P(1,x)$ gives $$f(f(x))^2=f(1)^2f(x)\implies f(x)=\left(\frac{f(f(x))}{f(1)}\right)^2$$Since $f$ maps $\mathbb{Q}_{>0}$ to $\mathbb{Q}_{>0}$, we must have $f(x)$ is a perfect square of a rational number for all $x\in \mathbb{Q}_{>0}$. Now assume the claim is true for some $n=k\geq 1$. Because of the assumption, we can set a function $g(x)$ such that $g(x)^{2^k}=f(x)$ over the same domain and range. We have $$g^{2^k}(x)=\left(\frac{g^{2^k}(f(x))}{g^{2^k}(1)}\right)^2=\left(\frac{g(f(x))}{g(1)}\right)^{2^{k+1}}$$ Taking the $2^k$th root gives $$g(x)=\left(\frac{g(f(x))}{g(1)}\right)^2$$ Since $g(x)$ is over $\mathbb{Q}_{>0}$, the LHS must be a perfect square of a rational for all $x$. Due to the definition of $g$ this means that $f(x)$ is a perfect $2^{k+1}$th power of some function over the positive rationals as well, completing the induction. style="vertical-align: -1px" width="11" height="14" >t<\/span>h, <img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/3\/9\/5\/3958e984d2597b35da7975f4503cdcf965a777f7.png" class="latex" alt="$\ldots$" The only number satisfying this is <span style="white-space:pre;"><img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/d\/c\/e\/dce34f4dfb2406144304ad0d6106c5382ddd1446.png" class="latex" alt="$1$" style="vertical-align: -1px" width="11" height="14" >,<\/span> so <img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/2\/2\/4\/224ae5ff2e22575ba5a41f26a68c2c5fdc0d502d.png" class="latex" alt="$f(x)=1$" style="vertical-align: -5px" width="71" height="20" > is the only solution.","post_canonical":"Let $P(x,y)$ denote the assertion.\\
$$P(\frac{1}{f(y)},y) \rightarrow f(1)=f(\frac{1}{f(y)})^2f(y)$$
Plug $y=1$ into this to get that $f(\frac{1}{f(1))}=1$.
Let $a=\frac{1}{f(1)}$. Substituting $(x,a)$ yields $f(x^2)=f(x)^2$. This allows us to rewrite the given FE as $f(xf(y))^2=f(x)^2f(y)$. This implies that $f(y)$ is always a square. This means that we can write $f(x)=f_1(x)^2$ for some $f_1$. substituting this in we get $f_1(xf_1(y)^2)^4=f_1(x)^4f_1(y)^2$. Now we can take the square root of this equation, and again find that $f_1(y)$ must be a square for all $y$. Repeating this argument, $f(y)$ has to be a perfect $2$nd, $4$th, $\ldots$ power. Let $c = f(1)$, and denote by $P(x, y)$ the assertion $f(x^2f(y)^2) = f(x)^2f(y)$. Then consider the following:
\begin{align*}
P(1\/c, 1): &\implies f \left(\frac{1}{c} \right) = 1 \\
P(x, 1\/c): &\implies f(x^2) = f(x)^2 \implies f(1) = 1.\\
&\implies f(y) = \left[\frac{f(xf(y))}{f(x)} \right]^2. \text{ Let } Q(x, y) \text{ denote this assertion.} \\
Q(1, y): &\implies f(y) = f(f(y))^2; \text{ and } f(x) = f(f(x))^2 \\
&\implies \frac{f(y)}{f(x)} = \left[\frac{f^{2}(y)}{f^{(2)}(x)} \right]^2 = \left[\frac{f^{(3)}(y)}{f^{(3)}(x)} \right]^4 = \cdots = \left[\frac{f^{(k)}(y)}{f^{(k)}(x)} \right]^{2^{k - 1}} = c_0. \\
\end{align*}
Now $c_0 = b^{2^{k - 1}}$, for some $b \in \mathbb{Q}_{>0}$, and in particular, if $f$ is to be well defined we require that $b \in [0, 1]$. Otherwise, when $k \rightarrow \infty$ we have that $b \rightarrow \infty$, which yields a contradiction.
Now, $f \neq 0$, so $c_0 = 1$ which gives us that $f(x) = f(y)$ for all pairs $x, y$. If now $f(x) = f(y) = d$, then plugging this into the initial equation yields \[d = d^3 \implies \boxed{f(x) = d = 1}, \] as desired. $\blacksquare$","username":"MagicalToaster53","reported":false,"is_thanked":false,"is_nothanked":false,"attachment":false,"thanks_received":0,"nothanks_received":0,"thankers":null,"deleted":false,"post_number":101,"post_time":1708818742,"num_edits":0,"post_format":"bbcode","last_edit_time":0,"last_editor_username":"","last_edit_reason":"","admin":false,"avatar":"\/\/avatar.artofproblemsolving.com\/avatar_1041605.jpg?t=1705112041","num_posts":146,"editable":false,"deletable":false,"show_from_start":false,"show_from_end":true},{"post_id":30107493,"topic_id":1876757,"poster_id":785179,"post_rendered":"We claim that answer is <span style="white-space:pre;"><img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/9\/2\/3\/9234877158199a1dc3fe6f80412ca70460f953fd.png" class="latex" alt="$f \equiv 1$" style="vertical-align: -3px" width="43" height="16" >,<\/span> which is easily checked. Consider taking $x = \frac{1}{f(y)}$ to fnd,
\begin{align*}
f(1) &= f(y) \cdot f\left(\frac{1}{f(y)} \right)^2
\end{align*}
Plugging in $y = 1$ this means,
\begin{align*}
f\left( \frac{1}{f(1)} \right) = 1
\end{align*}
However we may also note if we take $y = \frac{1}{f(1)}$,
\begin{align*}
f(x^2) = f(x)^2
\end{align*}
From here take this identity and plug it back into the original equation to find,
\begin{align*}
f(xf(y))^2 &= f(x)^2f(y) \iff \frac{f(xf(y))^2}{f(x)^2} = f(y)
\end{align*}
Then $f(x)$ is always the square of a rational number. However then as,
\begin{align*}
f(xf(y)) = f(x)\sqrt{f(y)}
\end{align*}
we require $f(y)$ to always be the fourth power of a rational number. However then this implies,
\begin{align*}
\frac{f(xf(y))}{f(x)} = \sqrt{f(y)}
\end{align*}
which in turn gives $\sqrt{f(y)}$ is the perfect fourth power of a rational, which forces $f(y)$ to be a perfect eighth power of a rational. Continuing this infinite descent $f(y)$ is a perfect power of a rational for each of the powers of $2$. Continuing by infinite descent, we gradually get that <img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/1\/7\/5\/175e1987c811b5eac74852135f77f7706836723c.png" class="latex" alt="$f(x)=F_{2^i}(x)^{2^i}$" style="vertical-align: -5px" width="123" height="24" > for all <img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/e\/f\/0\/ef086b1b06cb62ddb3ab8dc26705b2e28ad54ac1.png" class="latex" alt="$i \in \mathbb{N}$" style="vertical-align: -1px" width="41" height="13" ><br>
Thus, we can have no other solution, other than <span style="white-space:pre;"><img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/7\/5\/2\/752858f1c6a4fb424992217fb42327713ecc4826.png" class="latex" alt="$\boxed{f(x)\equiv 1}$" style="vertical-align: -10px" width="80" height="29" >,<\/span> and we&#039;re done! <img src="\/assets\/images\/smilies\/unsure.gif" alt=":maybe:" title=":maybe:" class="bbcode_smiley" \/>","post_canonical":"[color=#f00][b]Claim: [\/b][\/color]The only solution is $f(x)\equiv 1$.
Let $P(x,y)$ denote the assertion: $$f(x^2f(y)^2)=f(x)^2f(y)$$
$P\left( \frac{1}{f(1)},1\right) \implies f\left( \frac{1}{f(1)} \right)=1 \implies \exists \alpha$ such that $f(\alpha)=1$.
$$P(x,\alpha) \implies \boxed{f(x^2)=f(x)^2} \cdots(i)$$

$P(1,x^2) \implies f(1f(x)^2)^2=f(1)^2f(x)^2\implies f(f(x)^2)=f(1)f(x)$
$P(1,x) \implies f(f(x)^2)=f(1)^2.f(x)$
From these two equations, we get that $f(1)=1$.
$P(1,x) \implies f(x)=f(f(x))^2$

Let $F_i=f(f(f(..._{\text{i times}}))$
Therefore, $\forall x$, we have $f(x)=F_2(x)^2$.
Substituting this value of $f(x)$ in the original FE, we get:
$$F_2(x^2F_2(y)^2)=F_2(x)^2F_2(y)$$
Doing same thing recursively, we get that $F_2(x)=F_4(x)^2 \implies f(x)=F_4(x)^{2^2}$
Continuing by infinite descent, we gradually get that $f(x)=F_{2^i}(x)^{2^i}$ for all $i \in \mathbb{N}$
Thus, we can have no other solution, other than $\boxed{f(x)\equiv 1}$, and we're done! :maybe: ","username":"SilverBlaze_SY","reported":false,"is_thanked":false,"is_nothanked":false,"attachment":false,"thanks_received":0,"nothanks_received":0,"thankers":null,"deleted":false,"post_number":104,"post_time":1710405672,"num_edits":0,"post_format":"bbcode","last_edit_time":0,"last_editor_username":"","last_edit_reason":"","admin":false,"avatar":"\/\/avatar.artofproblemsolving.com\/avatar_1098226.png?t=1709784107","num_posts":65,"editable":false,"deletable":false,"show_from_start":false,"show_from_end":true},{"post_id":30245592,"topic_id":1876757,"poster_id":346741,"post_rendered":"Let the assertion be <span style="white-space:pre;"><img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/6\/d\/e\/6de7dc5f91f89a43911ae74626aa37d75d53ee9d.png" class="latex" alt="$P(x,y)$" style="vertical-align: -4px" width="55" height="18" >.<\/span> We claim the answer is <span style="white-space:pre;"><img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/2\/2\/4\/224ae5ff2e22575ba5a41f26a68c2c5fdc0d502d.png" class="latex" alt="$f(x)=1$" style="vertical-align: -5px" width="71" height="20" >,<\/span> which clearly works. <b><span style="color:#00f">Claim:<\/span><\/b> <img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/4\/4\/5\/4458e205a0c93dd06bcd58347d7e4ede6f2e0e9b.png" class="latex" alt="$f\left(\frac{1}{f(x)}\right)=\sqrt{\frac{f(1)}{f(x)}}$" style="vertical-align: -21px" width="163" height="56" ><br>

<b><span style="color:#00f">Claim:<\/span><\/b> <img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/f\/b\/0\/fb02e790c089602b3df4215091c5a5b17741d8d0.png" class="latex" alt="$f(x^2)=f(x)^2$" style="vertical-align: -4px" width="108" height="19" ><br>

<br>
When we substitute this back, we get:<br>

We claim the answer is $f(x)=1$, which clearly works. We now show it is the only possible solution.

[b][color=#00f]Claim:[\/color][\/b] $f\left(\frac{1}{f(x)}\right)=\sqrt{\frac{f(1)}{f(x)}}$
[i]Proof:[\/i] 
Using $P\left(\frac{1}{f(y)},y\right)$, we get:
\[f(1)=f\left(\frac{1}{f(y)}\right)^2\cdot f(y),\]
as desired $\square$

[b][color=#00f]Claim:[\/color][\/b] $f(x^2)=f(x)^2$
[i]Proof:[\/i]
We now use $P(1,y)$, which can be written as:
\[f(f(y)^2)=f(1)^2\cdot f(y).\]
By the previous claim, this is also:
\[f(f(y)^2)=\sqrt{f(1)}\cdot f(y).\]
Therefore:
\[f(1)^{3\/2}=1,\]
\[f(1)=1.\]
Then, if we use $P(x,1)$, we get the desired result $\square$

When we substitute this back, we get:
\[f(xf(y))^2=f(x)^2\cdot f(y).\]
This means that $f(y)=g(y)^2$, such that $g:\mathbb{Q}_{>0}\to \mathbb{Q}_{>0}$. Applying this, we get:
\[g(xg(y)^2)^4=g(x)^4g(y)^2,\]
\[g(xg(y)^2)^2=g(x)^2g(y).\]
As a result, $g$ must also be a square of a function $q: \mathbb{Q}_{>0}\to \mathbb{Q}_{>0}$, leading to an infinite descent. Therefore, $f$ must be a perfect $2^i$ power as $i$ approaches infinity, implying that $f$ must be $1$ Call a rational number $x$ an $n$-th power if $x=y^n$ for some rational number $y$.
$P(\frac{1}{f(1)},1) \implies f(1)=f(\frac{1}{f(1)})^2f(1) \implies f(\frac{1}{f(1)})=1$.
$P(1,\frac{1}{f(1)}) \implies f(1)=f(1)^2 \implies f(1)=1$.
$P(x,1) \implies f(x^2)=f(x)^2$.

We claim that for all positive integers $n$, $f(x)$ is a $2^n$-th power for all $x$. We will prove this by induction.
$P(\frac{1}{f(y)},y) \implies 1=f(\frac{1}{f(y)})^2f(y) \implies f(y)$ is a 2nd power for all $y$.
Now assume $f(x)$ is a $2^k$-th power for all $x$.
$P(x,y) \implies f(y)=\frac{f(xf(y))^2}{f(x)^2} \implies f(y)$ is a $2^{k+1}$-th power. This proves the claim.
It follows that $f(x)=1$ for all $x \in \mathbb{Q}_{>0}$. Claim: $f(x^2)=f(x)^2$
Plug in $(x,y)=(f(1)^{-2},1)$ giving $f(f(1)^{-2})=1$. Plug in $(x,y)=(x,f(1)^{-2})$ and the result follows. 

Now rewrite the assertion as, $$\frac{f(xf(y))^2}{f(x)^2}=f(y)$$ This implies that $f(y)$ is a second power. This is a contradiction, so that means <img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/b\/e\/5\/be554aaac6ffa7e62747f7b88d4b1a9a2edda74e.png" class="latex" alt="$\boxed{f(y)=1}$" style="vertical-align: -11px" width="81" height="32" > for all <span style="white-space:pre;"><img src="\/\/latex.artofproblemsolving.com\/0\/9\/2\/092e364e1d9d19ad5fffb0b46ef4cc7f2da02c1c.png" class="latex" alt="$y$" style="vertical-align: -4px" width="11" height="13" >.<\/span>","post_canonical":"Let $P(x,y)$ be the process of plugging in numbers for $x$ and $y$, respectively

Let $f(1)=c$

$P(\frac{1}{c},1)$ to get that $c=f(\frac{1}{c})^2c$

$f(\frac{1}{c})=1$

$P(y,\frac{1}{c})$ to get that $f(y^2)=f(y)^2$

$P(1,\frac{1}{c})$ to get that $c=c^2$, so $c=1$

$f(1)=1$

$P(1,y)$ to get $f(f(y)^2)=f(y)=f(f(y^2))$

$P(f(y),y)$ to get $f(f(y)^4)=f(f(y)^2)^2=f(f(y))^4=f(f(y))^2f(y)$

$f(y)=f(f(y))^2$

Let $f(a)=b$

$f(a)=f(f(a))^2=f(b)^2=b$

$f(b)=\sqrt{b}$

$f(\sqrt{b})=\sqrt[4]{b}$

...

If this goes on forever and $b \neq 1$, then one of the outputs of the function will eventually become irrational. Denote the given assertion as $P(x,y)$. 

Set $x = \tfrac{1}{f(1)}$ and $y = 1$:

\[f(1) = f\left( \frac{1}{f(1)^2}\right)f(1)\]
\[\implies f\left( \frac{1}{f(1)^2}\right) = 1.\]

Then, $P(x,\tfrac{1}{f(1)^2})$ gives

\[f(x^2) = f(x)^2.\]

Plug this into the original equation to see that

\[f(xf(y))^2 = f(x)^2f(y)\]

and then let $x = f(y)$, which simplifies to

\[f(f(y))^2 = f(y).\]

Hence, if $b$ is in the range of $f$ such that $f(a)=b$, we see that $f(f(a))^2 = f(b)^2 = f(a) = b$, so $\sqrt{b}$ is also in the range of $f$; infinite descent back to school time!  A new academic year is a new opportunity to train for the upcoming competition season.  Our <a href="https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/woot" class="bbcode_url" target="_blank">Worldwide Online Olympiad Training (WOOT)<\/a> classes begin the first week of September, so be sure to take advantage of the only program where you learn the skills and strategies necessary to succeed at the Olympiad Level.  All of this years International Mathematical Olympiad team members are AoPS alums and earned 5 gold medals and 1 silver medal, bringing home a first place finish!  Did you know that John Berman, this years IMO Team leader for the US, is also an AoPS alum!?<br>
<br>
Below is a list of the different WOOT programs and the competitions they cover.<br>

<ul class="bbcode_list">
<li><a href="https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/woot-math#level-1" class="bbcode_url" target="_blank">MathWOOT Level 1<\/a>:  Designed for AIME qualifiers ready to make the jump to Olympiads.<\/li>
<li><a href="https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/woot-math#level-2" class="bbcode_url" target="_blank">MathWOOT Level 2<\/a>:  Designed for Olympiad qualifiers ready to increase their scores in national and international Olympiads.<\/li>
<li><a href="https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/woot-code" class="bbcode_url" target="_blank">CodeWOOT<\/a>: USACO, IOI<\/li>
<li><a href="https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/woot-physics" class="bbcode_url" target="_blank">PhysicsWOOT<\/a>: USAPhO, SIN, IPhO, F=ma exam<\/li>
<li><a href="https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/woot-chem" class="bbcode_url" target="_blank">ChemWOOT<\/a>:  USNCO, IChO<\/li>
<\/ul>
For those not quite ready for CodeWOOT and are interested in coding competitions, check out our <b><a href="https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/cs-bronze" class="bbcode_url" target="_blank">brand new USACO Bronze course<\/a><\/b>!  To be eligible, students should already be comfortable enough with C++, Java, or Python to write simple programs using basic concepts like arrays, maps\/sets, if statements, and for loops. Note that either Java or Python is sufficient for the USACO Bronze and Silver levels (AoPS offers Python courses), but USACO Gold and above (and most programming contests) essentially require C++, and the IOI only supports C++.<br>
<br>
Keep in mind some other important dates coming up fast!
<ul class="bbcode_list">
<li><b>AMC 10\/12 competitions are right around the corner!<\/b> Take advantage of our accelerated <a href="https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/maa-amc10" class="bbcode_url" target="_blank">AMC 10<\/a> and <a href="https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/maa-amc12" class="bbcode_url" target="_blank">AMC 12<\/a> Problem Series courses which run from October until right before the competitions begin!  In these courses, you will learn test taking strategies and have the opportunity to take a practice exam - efficiently train to succeed in this years competitions!
<ul class="bbcode_list">
<li>The AMC 10A\/12A will be held on November 6th<\/li>
<li>The AMC 10B\/12B will be held on November 12th.<\/li>
<\/ul>
<\/li>
<li><a href="http:\/\/www.usamts.org\/" class="bbcode_url" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">USAMTS<\/a> (United States of America Mathematical Talent Search) will release their first round of problems the Friday after Labor day!  This contest is another method for qualifying for AIME. As an extra bonus, students can use the comments they receive on their USAMTS solutions to hone their skills for Olympiad level contests! The Round 1 problems will be available at <a href="http:\/\/www.usamts.org\/contest\/problems" class="bbcode_url" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">http:\/\/www.usamts.org\/contest\/problems<\/a>. Students should submit solutions to the Round 1 problems by mid-October.<\/li>
<li><a href="https:\/\/math.mit.edu\/research\/highschool\/primes\/program\/apply.html" class="bbcode_url" target="_blank">Admissions for MIT Primes<\/a> opens on October 1st!  MIT PRIMES is a free, year-long program, in which high school students work on individual and group research projects and participate in reading groups under the guidance of academic mentors. MIT PRIMES includes three sections: mathematics, computer science, and computational and physical biology.<\/li>
<\/ul>
<b>Were Hiring: Competition Computer Science Instructors<\/b><br>
AoPS Online is looking for freelance Computer Science Instructors with experience in high school coding competitions, either as participants or coaches. We hire instructors worldwide. If you&#039;re interested or know someone who might be, learn more and apply <a href="https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/careers\/job\/6094563003_online_competition_computer_science_instructor" class="bbcode_url" target="_blank">here<\/a>.<br>
<br>
<b>Dont forget: Highlight your AoPS Education on LinkedIn!<\/b><br>
Many of you are beginning to build your education and achievements history on LinkedIn.  Now, you can showcase your courses from Art of Problem Solving (AoPS) directly on your LinkedIn profile!<br>
<br>
Whether you&#039;ve taken our classes at AoPS Online or AoPS Academies or reached the top echelons of our competition training with our Worldwide Online Olympiad Training (WOOT) program, you can now add your AoPS experience to the education section on your LinkedIn profile.<br>
<br>
Don&#039;t miss this opportunity to stand out and connect with fellow problem-solvers in the professional world and be sure to follow us at: <a href="https:\/\/www.linkedin.com\/school\/art-of-problem-solving\/mycompany\/" class="bbcode_url" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">https:\/\/www.linkedin.com\/school\/art-of-problem-solving\/mycompany\/<\/a>  Check out our job postings, too, if you are interested in either full-time, part-time, or internship opportunities!<br>

Sat &amp; Sun, Dec 14 - Dec 15 (4:00 - 7:00 pm ET\/1:00 - 4:00pm PT)","post_canonical":"Its back to school time!  A new academic year is a new opportunity to train for the upcoming competition season.  Our [url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/woot]Worldwide Online Olympiad Training (WOOT)[\/url] classes begin the first week of September, so be sure to take advantage of the only program where you learn the skills and strategies necessary to succeed at the Olympiad Level.  All of this years International Mathematical Olympiad team members are AoPS alums and earned 5 gold medals and 1 silver medal, bringing home a first place finish!  Did you know that John Berman, this years IMO Team leader for the US, is also an AoPS alum!?

Below is a list of the different WOOT programs and the competitions they cover.

[list][*][url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/woot-math#level-1]MathWOOT Level 1[\/url]:  Designed for AIME qualifiers ready to make the jump to Olympiads. 
[*][url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/woot-math#level-2]MathWOOT Level 2[\/url]:  Designed for Olympiad qualifiers ready to increase their scores in national and international Olympiads.
[*][url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/woot-code]CodeWOOT[\/url]: USACO, IOI
[*][url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/woot-physics]PhysicsWOOT[\/url]: USAPhO, SIN, IPhO, F=ma exam
[*][url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/woot-chem]ChemWOOT[\/url]:  USNCO, IChO[\/list]
For those not quite ready for CodeWOOT and are interested in coding competitions, check out our [b][url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/cs-bronze]brand new USACO Bronze course[\/b][\/url]!  To be eligible, students should already be comfortable enough with C++, Java, or Python to write simple programs using basic concepts like arrays, maps\/sets, if statements, and for loops. Note that either Java or Python is sufficient for the USACO Bronze and Silver levels (AoPS offers Python courses), but USACO Gold and above (and most programming contests) essentially require C++, and the IOI only supports C++.  

Keep in mind some other important dates coming up fast!
[list][*][b]AMC 10\/12 competitions are right around the corner![\/b] Take advantage of our accelerated [url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/maa-amc10]AMC 10[\/url] and [url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/maa-amc12]AMC 12[\/url] Problem Series courses which run from October until right before the competitions begin!  In these courses, you will learn test taking strategies and have the opportunity to take a practice exam - efficiently train to succeed in this years competitions!
[list]
[*]The AMC 10A\/12A will be held on November 6th 
[*]The AMC 10B\/12B will be held on November 12th.[\/list]
[*][url=http:\/\/www.usamts.org\/]USAMTS[\/url] (United States of America Mathematical Talent Search) will release their first round of problems the Friday after Labor day!  This contest is another method for qualifying for AIME. As an extra bonus, students can use the comments they receive on their USAMTS solutions to hone their skills for Olympiad level contests! The Round 1 problems will be available at [url=http:\/\/www.usamts.org\/contest\/problems]www.usamts.org\/contest\/problems[\/url]. Students should submit solutions to the Round 1 problems by mid-October.
[*][url=https:\/\/math.mit.edu\/research\/highschool\/primes\/program\/apply.html]Admissions for MIT Primes[\/url] opens on October 1st!  MIT PRIMES is a free, year-long program, in which high school students work on individual and group research projects and participate in reading groups under the guidance of academic mentors. MIT PRIMES includes three sections: mathematics, computer science, and computational and physical biology.[\/list]

A new academic year is a new opportunity to train for the upcoming competition season.  Our <a href="https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/woot" class="bbcode_url">Worldwide Online Olympiad Training (WOOT)<\/a> classes begin the first week of September, so be sure to take advantage of the only program where you learn the skills and strategies necessary to succeed at the Olympiad Level. [list][*]<a href="https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/woot-math#level-1" class="bbcode_url">MathWOOT Level 1<\/a>:  Designed for AIME qualifiers ready to make the jump to Olympiads.<br>

Note that either Java or Python is sufficient for the USACO Bronze and Silver levels (AoPS offers Python courses), but USACO Gold and above (and most programming contests) essentially require C++, and the IOI only supports C++.<br>

Keep in mind some other important dates coming up fast!<br>
[list][*]<b>AMC 10\/12 competitions are right around the corner!<\/b> Take advantage of our accelerated <a href="https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/maa-amc10" class="bbcode_url">AMC 10<\/a> and <a href="https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/maa-amc12" class="bbcode_url">AMC 12<\/a> Problem Series courses which run from October until right before the competitions begin!  In these courses, you will learn test taking strategies and have the opportunity to take a practice exam - efficiently train to succeed in this years competitions!<br>
[list]<br>
[*]The AMC 10A\/12A will be held on November 6th<br>
[*]The AMC 10B\/12B will be held on November 12th.[\/list]<br>
[*]<a href="http:\/\/www.usamts.org\/" class="bbcode_url" rel="nofollow">USAMTS<\/a> (United States of America Mathematical Talent Search) will release their first round of problems the Friday after Labor day!  This contest is another method for qualifying for AIME. As an extra bonus, students can use the comments they receive on their USAMTS solutions to hone their skills for Olympiad level contests! The Round 1 problems will be available at <a href="http:\/\/www.usamts.org\/contest\/problems" class="bbcode_url" rel="nofollow">http:\/\/www.usamts.org\/contest\/problems<\/a>. Students should submit solutions to the Round 1 problems by mid-October.<br>
[*]<a href="https:\/\/math.mit.edu\/research\/highschool\/primes\/program\/apply.html" class="bbcode_url">Admissions for MIT Primes<\/a> opens on October 1st!  MIT PRIMES is a free, year-long program, in which high school students work on individual and group research projects and participate in reading groups under the guidance of academic mentors. MIT PRIMES includes three sections: mathematics, computer science, and computational and physical biology.[\/list]<br>

<script>
	// Try to prevent sending email multiple times
	var last_email = '';

	$( function() {
		$( ".aops-contact" ).on( "click", displayFooterEmailDialog );

		$( "body" ).on( "click", "#footer-submit-btn", submitFooterEmail );
		$( "body" ).on( "click", "#footer-cancel-btn",  cancelFooterEmail );
	} );

	function displayFooterEmailDialog() {
		AoPS.Ui.Modal.show( {
			body : document.getElementById( "footer-tpl-email-dialog" ).innerHTML,
			max_width : '600px',
			max_height : '100%',
			scrollable : true
		} );
	}


	/**
	 * Submit email
	 *
	 * Note: Copy does not work in non-production mode because Mailer::quick() is
	 * used which does not do force send.
	 */
	function submitFooterEmail() {
		var obj = {
			action : "email",
			email : $( "#email" ).val(),
			subject : $( "#subject" ).val(),
			message : $( "#message" ).val(),
			copy : $( "#email-copy" ).is( ":checked" )
		};

		if ( obj.email === last_email ) {
			return;
		}
		last_email = obj.email;

		// If this still happens multiple times, we have
		// something wrong with the internal ajax call.
		AoPS.Ui.Modal.closeAllModals();
		$.post( "/m/contact/ajax.php", obj, function( data ) {
			if ( data.error_code ) {
				$( "#email-error" ).html( data.error_msg ? data.error_msg : data.error_code ).show();
			} else {
				AoPS.Ui.Modal.show( {
					body : document.getElementById( "footer-tpl-email-confirm" ).innerHTML,
					max_width : '600px',
					max_height : '100%',
					scrollable : true
				} );
			}
		}, "json" );
	}
	function cancelFooterEmail() {
		AoPS.Ui.Modal.closeAllModals();
	}
</script>
